  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_bd2b9c12-9715-4939-b441-44b38a58a71d'
      });

But King Aiken's rule is precarious, for the Tanu's twisted brethren are secretly maneuvering to bring about his downfall. Worse, Aiken is about to confront a man of incredibly powerful talents who nearly overthrew a galactic rule. He is Marc Remillard. Call him...The Adversary.</span>

<a href="/questions/1278047-i-don-t-understand-the-ending-why-did"><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[i don't understand the ending. Why did Marc let his kids go? Is the idea that, when Elizabeth opens up to him, he's able to see that she is the one who can cure him and be his partner in creating Mental Man? That's the only thing i can think of but I don't see the bread crumbs for this <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></a>

<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/9887533-georgina"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1338398260i/9887533._UX100_CR0,5,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/9887533-georgina">Georgina</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<div class='spoiler'>
This answer contains spoilers…
<span class='spoilerAnswerText'>
<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  
    Yes it was a very abrupt change of heart with little explanation. I think he discovered the Tanu homeworld and fell in love with Elizabeth and discove<a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">…more</a>
    Yes it was a very abrupt change of heart with little explanation. I think he discovered the Tanu homeworld and fell in love with Elizabeth and discovered unity with her which made him more considerate of his Childrens wishes and the work they had done on the black torc babies made him see the potential for Mental man in the Tanu....so...yeah I think he was planning to turn the Tanu into Mental men. But why he decided to give up on his own DNA....maybe by falling in love he was able to forgive himself and then will be able to grow his testes back? I don't know, it was a disappointing conclusion to what had been a good series. Left more questions than answers and seemed to wrap up too quick in the last 30 pages.<a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">(less)</a>
  
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>
</span>
</div>
</span>

<div id="review_343034827" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/343034827" />
    <a title="Wanda" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9246169-wanda"><img alt="Wanda" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528903039p2/9246169.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/343034827?book_show_action=true">Jun 05, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Wanda" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Wanda" href="/user/show/9246169-wanda">Wanda</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780345314222" href="/book/show/1281119.The_Adversary">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=female-authors">female-authors</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=reading-project">reading-project</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=speculative-fiction">speculative-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=read-in-2015">read-in-2015</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer343034827" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4423768064243089748">My least favourite of the 4 Pliocene Exile books. It took me a while to get into this one, although eventually I found myself back into the flow. I have to admire the intricate nature of May’s plot and how it all ties together eventually. I did find that the sheer number of characters (not all of whom I remembered well) was part of the reason that it was slow going in the beginning. Also, the involvement of Marc Remillard was tiring to me—I was much more interested in the other humans, the Tanu </span>
  <span id="freeText4423768064243089748" style="display:none">My least favourite of the 4 Pliocene Exile books. It took me a while to get into this one, although eventually I found myself back into the flow. I have to admire the intricate nature of May’s plot and how it all ties together eventually. I did find that the sheer number of characters (not all of whom I remembered well) was part of the reason that it was slow going in the beginning. Also, the involvement of Marc Remillard was tiring to me—I was much more interested in the other humans, the Tanu and the Firvulag and their various plots and plans. Looking back, I realize that there was much less attention paid to the ancient environment and extinct animals in this book than in the others, and since that was one of the best parts of the series for me, it stands to reason that this book would be less appealing. I also missed the firey Felice and so many others who perished en route to this installment of the tale. (Perhaps George R.R. Martin learned a thing or two about killing off beloved characters from Ms. May?)<br /><br />A satisfactory conclusion to a really good tale. Many of my questions have been answered, although I think Ms. May has left herself some wiggle room to continue on at some future point, should she hear the Many Colored Land calling to her again.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4423768064243089748" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer3390521952799507962">George R.R. Martin can suck it. Ten years before Martin started his yet unfinished Song of Ice and Fire, May's four Pliocene Exile books were published at a rate of one every year between 1981 and 1984. And they are epic. Dense and filled with grand world-building concepts and vocabulary (although tinged with a bit of anachronism in places, mostly in some out-of-date ethnic clichés), The Adversary was a solid ending to a science fiction story set six million years ago. Rest in peace, Ms. May - y</span>
  <span id="freeText3390521952799507962" style="display:none">George R.R. Martin can suck it. Ten years before Martin started his yet unfinished Song of Ice and Fire, May's four Pliocene Exile books were published at a rate of one every year between 1981 and 1984. And they are epic. Dense and filled with grand world-building concepts and vocabulary (although tinged with a bit of anachronism in places, mostly in some out-of-date ethnic clichés), The Adversary was a solid ending to a science fiction story set six million years ago. Rest in peace, Ms. May - your books still bring me joy after all these years.</span>

        <a title="Sharon Reamer" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sharon Reamer" href="/user/show/9691874-sharon-reamer">Sharon Reamer</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9691874-sharon-reamer?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer615170242" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10692355734477046549">The overall series deserves four stars, even though I've given the third and fourth book three stars. I enjoyed the continuity, the exquisite world-building, enhanced by the cool geological descriptions. The detail of the survival of the humans in the Pliocene and their various trades and specialties was also rendered more than believable. I sometimes felt I was there, with them, in the Pliocene Exile. <br/><br/>However, the telepathic powers plot device overshadowed all of the good things, pushing my ove</span>
  <span id="freeText10692355734477046549" style="display:none">The overall series deserves four stars, even though I've given the third and fourth book three stars. I enjoyed the continuity, the exquisite world-building, enhanced by the cool geological descriptions. The detail of the survival of the humans in the Pliocene and their various trades and specialties was also rendered more than believable. I sometimes felt I was there, with them, in the Pliocene Exile. <br /><br />However, the telepathic powers plot device overshadowed all of the good things, pushing my overall enjoyment of the series down somewhat. I felt it got out of hand and would have liked a more 'mundane' solution to some of the problems between the different races: human, Tanu and Firvulag (including Howlers), and some conclusions about the mixed bloods influencing human evolution - that was never really satisfactorily explained (or I missed it).<br /><br />Many of my problems with the telepathic nature of the various antagonists and protagonists was that some things were just 'too easily' solved because of this. Character A has an insurmountable problem that Character B helps him/her solve using his/her superior telepathic (insert specific type of power here) skills.<br /><br />The series ended rather calmly, but it did have a solid ending so I can't complain too much.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10692355734477046549" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780330280310" href="/book/show/1018533.The_Adversary">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43250213-prcardi?shelf=series">series</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43250213-prcardi?shelf=pg-13">pg-13</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43250213-prcardi?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43250213-prcardi?shelf=time-travel">time-travel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43250213-prcardi?shelf=paranormal">paranormal</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1781184201" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18418601562530500335">Storyline: 2/5<br/>Characters: 2/5<br/>Writing Style: 2/5<br/>World: 2/5<br/><br/>This series has the distinction of making every subsequent volume worse than the one before. <br/><br/>Despite having 1,748 pages in which to develop the Pliocene, I never felt that I got to know the Many-Colored Land. We were immersed in politics and war, distracted with minor romances and side-quests, and pelted with PK technobabble, Latin and French, but the only feeling I got for the land was that the mountains were taller and the fauna more sau</span>
  <span id="freeText18418601562530500335" style="display:none">Storyline: 2/5<br />Characters: 2/5<br />Writing Style: 2/5<br />World: 2/5<br /><br />This series has the distinction of making every subsequent volume worse than the one before. <br /><br />Despite having 1,748 pages in which to develop the Pliocene, I never felt that I got to know the Many-Colored Land. We were immersed in politics and war, distracted with minor romances and side-quests, and pelted with PK technobabble, Latin and French, but the only feeling I got for the land was that the mountains were taller and the fauna more saurian. I don't think the book would have been all that different were it to have taken place in the Galactic Milieu or in Duat. <br /><br />May set up the Pliocene Exile well; there was great potential at the end of book one. Unfortunately, she suspended her worldbuilding efforts thereafter and it was more of a court intrigue and warring factions series thereafter. I think May was at her best when starting new ideas. She very rarely saw them through to satisfactory completion however and instead jumped on to something new. It was clear by midway through this final volume, that the Adversary was as much a bridge to the spin-off Galactic Milieu trilogy as it was a finale to the Saga of the Pliocene Exile series. </span>
  <a data-text-id="18418601562530500335" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_530281502" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/530281502" />
    <a title="Roddy Williams" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2160463-roddy-williams"><img alt="Roddy Williams" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1358104537p2/2160463.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/530281502?book_show_action=true">Feb 08, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Roddy Williams" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Roddy Williams" href="/user/show/2160463-roddy-williams">Roddy Williams</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780330280310" href="/book/show/1018533.The_Adversary">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2160463-roddy-williams?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2160463-roddy-williams?shelf=time">time</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2160463-roddy-williams?shelf=psi-powers">psi-powers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2160463-roddy-williams?shelf=human-origins">human-origins</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2160463-roddy-williams?shelf=mythology">mythology</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer530281502&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer530281502" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6977831854875406104">‘The Firvulag are rising, while the children of the metapsychic rebels race to reopen the time-gate, the sole escape route back into the Galactic Milieu.<br/>Now the adversary takes up his destined role in the power play… Marc Remillard, defeated leader of the metapsychic rebellion determined to keep the time-gate sealed and to create a new race from his own offspring. Will he aid the Firvulag or bring succour to Aiken, when the day of the Grand Tourney comes, when the Tanu and the humans meet the Fi</span>
  <span id="freeText6977831854875406104" style="display:none">‘The Firvulag are rising, while the children of the metapsychic rebels race to reopen the time-gate, the sole escape route back into the Galactic Milieu.<br />Now the adversary takes up his destined role in the power play… Marc Remillard, defeated leader of the metapsychic rebellion determined to keep the time-gate sealed and to create a new race from his own offspring. Will he aid the Firvulag or bring succour to Aiken, when the day of the Grand Tourney comes, when the Tanu and the humans meet the Firvulag in the last great contest of the exile world…’<br /><br />Blurb from the 1984 Pan paperback edition<br /><br /><br />May rounds off her Pliocene quadrology with panache in ‘The Adversary’<br />Aiken Drum is attempting to hold his kingdom together while the Firvulag are rising, convinced by ancient Duat prophecy that the Nightfall War is about to begin, the final battle to oblivion between the Tanu and the Firvulag.<br />The central figure of the prophecy, The Adversary, is seen as Marc Remillard, who has sailed from his exile in Pliocene Florida back to Europe in order to prevent his children reopening the Time Gate and escaping back to the Twenty-Second Century.<br />As a result of having nearly been killed by Felice when she teleported to America, Marc is slowly learning how to ‘D-Jump’ himself, and begins to appear to the Metapsychic Grandmaster Elizabeth Orme where he helps her to ‘cure’ the black-torc babies (i.e. babies who cannot adjust to the mind-enhancing torc).<br />It transpires that a metapsychic programme is able to not only cure the children but raise them to metapsychic operancy.<br />Once again, May manages to combine the fantasy settings with complete 22nd Century science quite seamlessly, and one has to ask how much she was influenced by the Science fantasy boom of the Seventies and writers such as Moorcock, M John Harrison and Jack Vance.<br />There are certainly echoes of their work here. Where these writers often set their civilisations of decadent technology on a Far Future Earth, May takes us back to the Pliocene of six million years ago, but the trappings are the same. The Tanu and the Firvulag are, after all, merely elves and goblins, trolls and ogres with a technology so advanced it appears to be magic. Where May triumphs is in linking her world so directly to our near future and creating a structure in which the narrative returns to the future and, to a certain extent, comes full circle to where it began.<br />Finally, it is revealed, although it has been hinted at within previous volumes, that the human race are descendants of all three races, which is why Humanity ends up possessing such a huge metapsychic potential.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6977831854875406104" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer10252259662216655049"><a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[<br/><br/>I thought this book was pretty average. It felt too long, but in spite of its length it left a couple of plot strings dangling badly. One was that she took a major character, Felice, out of the<br/>story but rather than killing her left her in sort of limbo. There was also this subplot about two infants that apparently were destined to do something but never did. These were dwelled on in book 3 at considerable length but never mentioned at all in book 4.   <span id="freeText10252259662216655049" style="display:none"><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br /><br />I thought this book was pretty average. It felt too long, but in spite of its length it left a couple of plot strings dangling badly. One was that she took a major character, Felice, out of the<br />story but rather than killing her left her in sort of limbo. There was also this subplot about two infants that apparently were destined to do something but never did. These were dwelled on in book 3 at considerable length but never mentioned at all in book 4. The author may have been trying to tie this series into another. If so, the connection was not obvious and I never read it. <br /><br />The author's style was average, not impressing me one way or the other. I did like the setting and some of the characterizations. The story showed a lot of imagination but overall it was not a very satisifying read.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer15711241273944856988">I was surprised at how much I had forgotten about this book. I have to admit that the lengthy subplots involving the misadventures of Tony Wayland and the climb up Monte Rosa were probably the least interesting part of the series for me, which may have contributed to its forgettableness. But this also may have made it even more enjoyable as a reread - for example, I didn't remember what happened to Basil when he reached the top of the mountain, so that was a nice surprise. And to be honest I did</span>
  <span id="freeText15711241273944856988" style="display:none">I was surprised at how much I had forgotten about this book. I have to admit that the lengthy subplots involving the misadventures of Tony Wayland and the climb up Monte Rosa were probably the least interesting part of the series for me, which may have contributed to its forgettableness. But this also may have made it even more enjoyable as a reread - for example, I didn't remember what happened to Basil when he reached the top of the mountain, so that was a nice surprise. And to be honest I didn't recall exactly how everything sorted itself out at the end. The parts involving the strongest characters, Aiken, Elizabeth, and Marc, were the most engaging, bringing the central story to a solid and satisfying end for the Pliocine saga. Looking forward to rereading the Milieu books next.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer5851890038050723862">Satisfactory ending to an overall satisfactory science-fictionish epic. I found it hard to buy Marc as a real Abaddon (perhaps the books set prior to the series lay groundwork for that?), and I never really <i>got</i> Elizabeth, but May managed to slot her mythic pieces together without too heavily straining the characters, and there was an amusingly literal big bang to end it all.<br/><br/>I liked this series. Most of this was the concept -- men and fey living as gods in ancient times, wrought from technology a</span>
  <span id="freeText5851890038050723862" style="display:none">Satisfactory ending to an overall satisfactory science-fictionish epic. I found it hard to buy Marc as a real Abaddon (perhaps the books set prior to the series lay groundwork for that?), and I never really <i>got</i> Elizabeth, but May managed to slot her mythic pieces together without too heavily straining the characters, and there was an amusingly literal big bang to end it all.<br /><br />I liked this series. Most of this was the concept -- men and fey living as gods in ancient times, wrought from technology and psychic powers, all stirred into a primeval myth that tugged at vague memories and presented similarities with a bunch of cultural artefacts. But it was competently executed too, and I enjoyed not having to worry about the author getting lost in their own world.</span>

<div id="review_357958" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/357958" />
    <a title="John Devlin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/34033-john-devlin"><img alt="John Devlin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1174519150p2/34033.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/357958?book_show_action=true">Mar 20, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="John Devlin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="John Devlin" href="/user/show/34033-john-devlin">John Devlin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer357958" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13751449798683073383">Simply the best space opera, and the best series of novels I've ever read. This is the first of the nine, and while the last three show signs of fatigue, these novels capture a cast of characters, and one in Marc Remillard, that are truly memorable. From the worlds and milieu May imagines to her evocative themes, the novels capture humanity with all its foibles and promise, and if you stick around for #6, you'll get the best plot twist in all of bookdom.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer1054705841139391289">I've finally found the kind of sci-fi I've been looking for! The kind of sci-fi that uses space and technology and made up sciences as plot devices. This is far from a perfect series and is certainly problematic. I feel this is a guilty pleasure for me in a way. There are so many things wrong with it but it's a book I enjoyed. It does handle more delicate topics and representation badly. Not to mention the excusing of abusers. I enjoyed it. Be wary going in as it's a product of its time.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2126925975" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17430546830047976494">Hoo boy. I wanted to be surprised and like <i>The Adversary</i>, but there was no way, for three huge reasons:<br/><br/>1) Abusers are, at best, treated as “well-meaning but complicated” and, at worst, excused without second thought.<br/>2) The scope becomes so large it’s impossible to support.<br/>3) Facts are artificially withheld from the reader to generate interest.<br/><br/>That first point is hard to swallow no matter what, but the last two could have been mitigated with good characterization and a world brimming with persona</span>
  <span id="freeText17430546830047976494" style="display:none">Hoo boy. I wanted to be surprised and like <i>The Adversary</i>, but there was no way, for three huge reasons:<br /><br />1) Abusers are, at best, treated as “well-meaning but complicated” and, at worst, excused without second thought.<br />2) The scope becomes so large it’s impossible to support.<br />3) Facts are artificially withheld from the reader to generate interest.<br /><br />That first point is hard to swallow no matter what, but the last two could have been mitigated with good characterization and a world brimming with personality, but the vibrancy of <i>The Many Colored World</i> is long gone. I guess I'm glad that I know how it ends, but <i>The Adversary</i> is so bad that I think it detracts from every book that preceded it.<br /><br />[I read old fantasy and sci-fi novels written by women authors in search of forgotten gems. See a full length review at <a href="https://www.forfemfan.com/home/2020/2/10/the-adversary-by-julian-may" rel="nofollow noopener">forfemfan.com</a>]</span>

Shoot, that is a bit of a spoiler... <br/><br/>What I can say is that the books were extremely well-written.   <span id="freeText6991811031736384537" style="display:none">I try to avoid writing any spoilers in my reviews. So I can't write about the plot, other than to let you know that the story came to a suitable end. I can't write about the firvulag (I think that's what they were called?) and the Tanu. And hurley as the new tournament. Shoot, that is a bit of a spoiler... <br /><br />What I can say is that the books were extremely well-written. A bit heavy on the emotional sometimes, a bit heavy on the science sometimes, and (if I recall) a bit hard for a young teenager to comprehend at times. But I loved it back then, as I still do now. And that is probably the best thing I can say about this series.<br /><br />Thank you, Julian May, for sharing your creation with us. </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Bgn2/06sxSas6IeaRq8AL2XCFDqjk21ne5ObWXOdyRDuEczkkYAmfxE+M0fWf6s8rOZcQP3GHoBr9juuLWcTRQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('UQGBPwsHpnSkxB7v3+Fs0kw7VWCTnPYvaMSsfZDJJie5Gbsk1CtFLRkSqjJPMcfBhR8dGs3Jhch4oQyKzjP8cg==')})
  }
//]]>
